Given the complexity, we might look for a synthetic solution that uses properties of cyclic quadrilaterals and orthocenters.

Let's denote:

- Ω: circle with center M, radius r.
- Γ: circle with center N, radius R.
- A, B intersection points.
- C on Ω on line MN opposite N, D on Γ on line MN beyond N.
- P: circumcenter of triangle ACD. So PA = PC = PD.
- E = AP ∩ Ω (again), F = AP ∩ Γ (again).
- H = orthocenter of triangle PMN.

We want: line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF).

Observations: Since P is circumcenter of ACD, we have that A, C, D are on circle with center P. So P is the circumcenter. Also, note that M and N are centers of Ω and Γ. So perhaps there is a relation between P, M, N and the radical axis. Since A and B are intersections, the line AB is the radical axis of Ω and Γ, and it is perpendicular to MN.

Also, since P is the circumcenter of ACD, we have that the circle (ACD) has center P. The power of M and N with respect to this circle we computed: Power(M) = MC * MD = -r(d+R). Power(N) = NC * ND = -R(d+r). So both are negative, meaning M and N are inside circle (ACD). So the circle (ACD) contains M and N in its interior. That is interesting: The circle through A and the two points where the line of centers meets the circles (the "outer" intersections) contains the centers inside it.

Now consider triangle PMN. Its orthocenter H. Since M and N lie on the line MN (horizontal), and P is somewhere, we have H on the vertical line through P, with y-coordinate as computed. There might be a relation with the circle (ACD). Since M and N are inside that circle, and P is its center, the altitudes from M and N in triangle PMN might intersect the circle at some points.

We might also consider the circumcircle of triangle ACD (center P). Since A, C, D are on that circle, and we have points E and F on line AP. Note that E is the second intersection of AP with Ω, and F is second intersection of AP with Γ. So we have a configuration: Two circles Ω and Γ intersect at A and B. A line through A (AP) meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So the points A, E, B, and A, F, B are on the circles. There is a known fact: The line through the orthocenter of triangle formed by the two centers and the circumcenter of ACD might be related to the Miquel point of the complete quadrilateral formed by lines AB, CD, and something? Hmm.

Maybe we can try to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is the polar of something with respect to circle (BEF). Or that H is the center of a homothety that sends the circle (BEF) to the line through H parallel to AP? Alternatively, we can use coordinate geometry to prove the tangency condition. Since it's an olympiad problem, coordinates might be acceptable if we can simplify elegantly. We can set up coordinate system as we did, with M=(0,0), N=(d,0), C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). Then compute everything in terms of d, r, R. Then we need to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of BEF. That is a purely algebraic statement, which we can attempt to verify by computing the equation of the circle and the distance from its center to the line. However, the expressions might be heavy but manageable with symbolic simplification. We can use the fact that many quantities are symmetric and we can factor out common factors.

Let's attempt an algebraic proof.

We have:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0) with d>0.
r < R, and d > R-r, d < R+r.

Define:
x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d)
y_A = √(r^2 - x_A^2) > 0.
Then B = (x_A, -y_A).

C = (-r, 0)
D = (d+R, 0)

Midpoint of CD: ((d+R - r)/2, 0) = (x_P, 0). So x_P = (d+R-r)/2.

We also have y_P from circumcenter of ACD. Compute y_P using formula: For triangle ACD, the circumcenter P is intersection of perpendicular bisectors. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = x_P. So P = (x_P, y_P). We need y_P.

The circumcenter can also be found as the point equidistant from A, C, D. So we have:

PA^2 = PC^2 = PD^2.

PC^2 = (x_P - (-r))^2 + (y_P - 0)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.
PD^2 = (x_P - (d+R))^2 + y_P^2 = (x_P - d - R)^2 + y_P^2.

Since x_P = (d+R-r)/2, we have:
x_P + r = (d+R-r)/2 + r = (d+R+r)/2.
x_P - d - R = (d+R-r)/2 - d - R = (d+R-r -2d -2R)/2 = (-d -R -r)/2 = -(d+R+r)/2.

So (x_P + r)^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2, and (x_P - d - R)^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 as well. So indeed PC^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + y_P^2, and PD^2 same. So PC = PD automatically; we just need PA^2 = PC^2.

Thus:
PA^2 = (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2. (Equation 1)

Let's denote:
Δ = (d+R+r)/2. Then x_P + r = Δ, and x_P - d - R = -Δ. So PC^2 = Δ^2 + y_P^2.

Now we can compute x_P - x_A =? As we did: x_P - x_A = ((R-r)(d+R+r))/(2d) = ((R-r)(2Δ))/(2d) = ((R-r)Δ)/d. So x_P - x_A = ((R-r)Δ)/d.

Then Equation 1 becomes:
((R-r)Δ/d)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = Δ^2 + y_P^2.

Expand:
((R-r)^2 Δ^2)/d^2 + (y_P^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2) = Δ^2 + y_P^2.

Cancel y_P^2:
((R-r)^2 Δ^2)/d^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = Δ^2.

Thus:
-2y_P y_A = Δ^2 - y_A^2 - ((R-r)^2 Δ^2)/d^2.

So:
2y_P y_A = y_A^2 + ((R-r)^2 Δ^2)/d^2 - Δ^2.

But note that y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2. And we have expression for y_A^2 in terms of d, r, R: y_A^2 = ((d+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (d-r)^2) / (4d^2). And Δ = (d+R+r)/2. So we can compute the RHS.

We also have that from the circles, we have relation: (x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) =? Might be useful.

Alternatively, we can solve for y_P directly by using the fact that P is the circumcenter, so y_P can be expressed as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. We already have y_P = y_A/2 - (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A)/(2y_A). That might be simpler to keep.

Now line AP: direction vector v = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A) = ( ((R-r)Δ)/d, y_P - y_A ). We have y_P - y_A = -(y_A^2+product)/(2y_A) = - (y_A^2 + (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A))/(2y_A). But note that (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A) =? We can compute that product in terms of Δ and maybe y_A.

We might also find that v is parallel to some vector related to (R-r, something). Actually from x_P - x_A = ((R-r)Δ)/d, and y_P - y_A =? Possibly the slope is something like - (something) * (R+r)/? Not sure.

Now H = orthocenter of triangle PMN. We have M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(x_P, y_P). As derived, H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P). So H = (x_P, h_y) where h_y = x_P(d-x_P)/y_P.

Now line L through H parallel to AP: direction v. So L is given by (x,y) = (x_P, h_y) + t v.

We need to show that L is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. Let's denote circle (BEF). We need to find its equation and show that the distance from its center to L equals its radius. Alternatively, we could show that the line L is the polar of something, or that L is the Simson line of H with respect to triangle BEF? Not clear.

Maybe we can find that the circumcircle of BEF is actually the circle with diameter something? Since B is the reflection of A across MN, and E and F lie on line AP, maybe there is a relation: The circle through B, E, F is symmetric to circle through A, E, F? Not exactly.

Alternatively, we can compute coordinates of E and F in terms of s_E and s_F.

Parameterize AP: A + s v, where v = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). So:

x(s) = x_A + s (x_P - x_A) = x_A + s Δx, where Δx = x_P - x_A = ((R-r)Δ)/d.
y(s) = y_A + s (y_P - y_A) = y_A + s Δy, where Δy = y_P - y_A.

We have that A is at s=0, and P is at s=1 (since P = (x_P, y_P) = (x_A+Δx, y_A+Δy)). So indeed s=1 gives P.

Now circle Ω: x^2+y^2 = r^2. Since A lies on Ω, we have x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2. The second intersection E corresponds to s = s_E such that:

(x_A + s Δx)^2 + (y_A + s Δy)^2 = r^2.

But since x_A^2+y_A^2 = r^2, we have:

2s (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) + s^2 (Δx^2+Δy^2) = 0 => s ( 2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) + s (Δx^2+Δy^2) ) = 0.

So s=0 (A) and s = s_E = -2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) / (Δx^2+Δy^2). So E = A + s_E v.

Similarly, for circle Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 = R^2. Since A lies on Γ, we have (x_A-d)^2+y_A^2 = R^2. For point on AP: x = x_A + s Δx, y = y_A + s Δy. Then:

(x-d)^2+y^2 = (x_A - d + s Δx)^2 + (y_A + s Δy)^2 = (x_A-d)^2 + 2s((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) + s^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) = R^2 + 2s((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) + s^2(Δx^2+Δy^2). Set equal to R^2, so:

2s((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) + s^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) = 0 => s (2((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) + s (Δx^2+Δy^2)) = 0. So s=0 (A) and s = s_F = -2 ((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) / (Δx^2+Δy^2). So F = A + s_F v.

Thus we have E and F expressed in terms of s_E and s_F.

Now B = (x_A, -y_A). So triangle BEF: points B, E, F.

Now we want to show that line L (through H parallel to AP) is tangent to circumcircle of BEF. That is, the line L touches that circle.

Set up:

M = (0,0)
N = (d,0)
r, R, d with conditions.

We have:
Δ = (d+R+r)/2
x_P = (d+R-r)/2

Δx = x_P - x_A = ((R-r)Δ)/d

We also need y_P and y_A. Let's compute y_A^2 and y_P.

We have y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2.

x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d)

Compute x_A^2 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2/(4d^2)

Thus y_A^2 = r^2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)^2/(4d^2) = (4d^2 r^2 - (d^2+r^2-R^2)^2)/(4d^2). As before.

Also, we have Δ = (d+R+r)/2, so 2Δ = d+R+r.

Now, we need to compute s_E and s_F.

Compute x_A Δx + y_A Δy. But we need Δy = y_P - y_A. We have y_P from formula: y_P = y_A/2 - (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A)/(2y_A). So Δy = -y_A/2 - (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A)/(2y_A) = -(y_A^2 + (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A))/(2y_A). So Δy = - (y_A^2 + Q)/(2y_A), where Q = (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A). So then:

x_A Δx + y_A Δy = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2.

Thus s_E = -2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) / (Δx^2+Δy^2) = -2( x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2 ) / (Δx^2+Δy^2) = -2x_A Δx + (y_A^2+Q) / (Δx^2+Δy^2). So s_E = (y_A^2+Q) / (Δx^2+Δy^2) - 2x_A Δx.

But maybe we can simplify using geometry: Since A is on both circles, and P is the circumcenter of ACD, there might be relation: The power of A with respect to circle (ACD) is 0 (since A on it). Also, the line AP is the line through A and the center. So the reflection of A across P? Not.

Alternatively, we could compute E and F directly in terms of d, r, R by solving the intersections.

Similarly, for Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2=R^2, and A on Γ, so s_F = -2 ((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) / (Δx^2+Δy^2).

Thus s_F = s_E + 2d Δx / (v·v). So we have expressions.

Now we need circumcircle of B, E, F. Since B, E, F are points, and we have coordinates, we can compute the circle equation. But perhaps we can find that the circle (BEF) is actually the image of the line MN under some transformation? Or that it is the circle with diameter something? Let's compute for the numeric example: r=1, R=2, d=1.5. We have A=(-0.25,0.9682), x_P=1.25, y_P=-0.9669, so v=(1.5, -1.9351). Compute v·v = 1.5^2+1.9351^2 = 2.25+3.745=5.995. Midpoint of BE: ((0.875-0.25)/2=0.3125, (-0.4828-0.9682)/2=-0.7255). Slope of BE: ( -0.4828+0.9682)/(0.875+0.25)=0.4854/1.125=0.4316. Perpendicular slope = -1/0.4316 = -2.315. Perpendicular slope = 1/0.4297=2.326. Equation: y+1.45135 = 2.326(x-0.875). Solve for intersection? That gives center. Let's solve numerically.

From first: y = -2.315(x-0.3125)-0.7255 = -2.315x+0.7228-0.7255 = -2.315x-0.0027.
From second: y = 2.326(x-0.875)-1.45135 = 2.326x-2.0335-1.45135 = 2.326x-3.48485.

Now line L through H parallel to AP. H = (x_P, h_y) = (1.25, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P) = (1.25, 1.25*(1.5-1.25)/(-0.9669)=1.25*0.25/(-0.9669)=0.3125/(-0.9669)=-0.3235). So H=(1.25, -0.3235). v=(1.5, -1.9351). Thus we can attempt a general algebraic proof. The algebra may simplify nicely if we use the expressions in terms of d, r, R, and also use the fact that s_E and s_F have specific forms.

Let's define:

Let a = d (distance between centers). Let r, R as given.

Define:

x_A = (a^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2a)
y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2 = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (a-r)^2)/(4a^2). So y_A = √(that).

We also have:

Δ = (a+R+r)/2
x_P = (a+R-r)/2

Δx = x_P - x_A = ((R-r)Δ)/a

We have y_P = y_A/2 - (x_A+r)(a+R-x_A)/(2y_A). And we also have Δy = y_P - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A), where Q = (x_A+r)(a+R-x_A). So v = (Δx, Δy). Compute v·v = Δx^2+Δy^2.

We can compute s_E = -2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) / (v·v). But note that x_A Δx + y_A Δy = x_A Δx + y_A Δy. Since Δy = - (y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A), we have y_A Δy = - (y_A^2+Q)/2. So x_A Δx + y_A Δy = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2.

Similarly, s_F = s_E + 2a Δx / (v·v).

Now we need the circumcircle of B, E, F. B = (x_A, -y_A). E = A + s_E v, F = A + s_F v.

Let’s denote t = s_E, and s = s_F. Then E = A + t v, F = A + s v.

So points: B, A+ t v, A+ s v.

We can find the circumcircle equation. Since v is direction of AP, and A, t, s are on line AP. Possibly we can use the fact that the circle through B, E, F is the image of the line through B'? But maybe we can compute the center and radius in terms of t and s, and then compute distance from H to that circle's center.

Alternatively, we can use the concept of power of a point: The line L through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF) iff the power of H with respect to that circle equals the square of the distance from H to the circle's center minus radius squared? Actually, for a point H, if a line through H is tangent, then the distance from the center to the line equals radius. But we can also use the fact that the line L is tangent to circle (BEF) iff the line L intersects the circle at exactly one point, which is equivalent to that the equation of the line and the circle have a double root. First, we need to compute Q = (x_A+r)(a+R-x_A). And also y_A^2.

We have x_A = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a). Then x_A+r = (a^2+r^2-R^2+2ar)/(2a) = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)/(2a) = ( (a+r-R)(a+r+R) )/(2a). And a+R-x_A = a+R - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (2a(a+R) - (a^2+r^2-R^2))/(2a) = (2a^2+2aR - a^2 - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = (a^2+2aR - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = ((a+R)^2 - r^2)/(2a) = ( (a+R-r)(a+R+r) )/(2a). So Q = (x_A+r)(a+R-x_A) = [ (a+r-R)(a+r+R) ] / (2a) * [ (a+R-r)(a+R+r) ] / (2a) = [ (a+r-R)(a+R-r) * (a+r+R)(a+R+r) ] / (4a^2). But (a+r+R)(a+R+r) = (a+r+R)^2 = (a+r+R)^2. And (a+r-R)(a+R-r) = (a + (r-R))(a + (R-r)) = a^2 - (r-R)^2 = a^2 - (R-r)^2. So Q = (a^2 - (R-r)^2) * (a+r+R)^2 / (4a^2). Good.

Now y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2 = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (a-r)^2) / (4a^2). Note that ((a+r)^2 - R^2) = (a+r-R)(a+r+R). And (R^2 - (a-r)^2) = (R - (a-r))(R + (a-r)) = (R - a + r)(R + a - r) = (r+R-a)(a+R-r). So y_A^2 = (a+r-R)(a+r+R) * (r+R-a)(a+R-r) / (4a^2). So y_A^2 = ((a+r-R)(r+R-a)) * ((a+r+R)(a+R-r)) / (4a^2). But (a+r-R)(r+R-a) = ( (r+R)^2 - a^2 ) = ( (r+R)^2 - a^2 ). And (a+r+R)(a+R-r) = ( (a+R)^2 - r^2 ) = ( (a+R)^2 - r^2 ). So y_A^2 = ((r+R)^2 - a^2) * ((a+R)^2 - r^2) / (4a^2). That's a symmetric form.

Now compute y_A^2+Q:

y_A^2+Q = [((r+R)^2 - a^2) * ((a+R)^2 - r^2) + (a^2 - (R-r)^2) * (a+r+R)^2] / (4a^2).

We also have Δx = ((R-r)Δ)/a, where Δ = (a+R+r)/2, so Δx = ((R-r)(a+R+r))/(2a). Good.

Now v·v = Δx^2+Δy^2. But Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So Δy^2 = (y_A^2+Q)^2/(4y_A^2). So v·v = Δx^2 + (y_A^2+Q)^2/(4y_A^2). We can write this as:

v·v = (4y_A^2 Δx^2 + (y_A^2+Q)^2) / (4y_A^2).

Also, we have s_E = -2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) / (v·v) = -2 (x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2) / (v·v) = (-2x_A Δx + (y_A^2+Q)) / (v·v). So s_E = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx) / (v·v). But note that x_A - a = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) - a = (a^2+r^2-R^2 -2a^2)/(2a) = (-a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = -(a^2+R^2-r^2)/(2a). That may be related to something.

Let’s denote:

Let A = (x_A, y_A), B = (x_A, -y_A). v = (Δx, Δy). t = s_E, s = s_F.

Then E = (x_A + t Δx, y_A + t Δy), F = (x_A + s Δx, y_A + s Δy).

We want the circumcenter O = (X, Y). Condition: OB^2 = OE^2 = OF^2.

So OB^2 = (X - x_A)^2 + (Y + y_A)^2.
OE^2 = (X - x_A - t Δx)^2 + (Y - y_A - t Δy)^2.
OF^2 = (X - x_A - s Δx)^2 + (Y - y_A - s Δy)^2.

Set OB^2 = OE^2 and OB^2 = OF^2.

From OB^2 = OE^2:
(X - x_A)^2 + (Y + y_A)^2 = (X - x_A - t Δx)^2 + (Y - y_A - t Δy)^2.
Expand:
(X - x_A)^2 + (Y + y_A)^2 = (X - x_A)^2 - 2tΔx(X - x_A) + t^2Δx^2 + (Y - y_A)^2 - 2tΔy(Y - y_A) + t^2Δy^2.
Cancel (X - x_A)^2:
(Y + y_A)^2 = -2tΔx(X - x_A) + t^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + (Y - y_A)^2 - 2tΔy(Y - y_A).
Now (Y + y_A)^2 - (Y - y_A)^2 = 4Y y_A.
So:
4Y y_A = -2tΔx(X - x_A) - 2tΔy(Y - y_A) + t^2 v·v.
Thus:
2Y y_A = -tΔx(X - x_A) - tΔy(Y - y_A) + (t^2/2) v·v.   (Eq1)

Similarly, from OB^2 = OF^2:
4Y y_A = -2sΔx(X - x_A) - 2sΔy(Y - y_A) + s^2 v·v.
Thus:
2Y y_A = -sΔx(X - x_A) - sΔy(Y - y_A) + (s^2/2) v·v.   (Eq2)

First, compute (X - x_A) = a/2 - x_A. We have x_A = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a). So a/2 - x_A = a/2 - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (a^2 - (a^2+r^2-R^2))/(2a) = ( -r^2 + R^2 )/(2a) = (R^2 - r^2)/(2a). So X - x_A = (R^2 - r^2)/(2a). That's a nice simple expression! Good.

Now also, we have Δx = ((R-r)Δ)/a. And Δ = (a+R+r)/2. So Δx = ((R-r)(a+R+r))/(2a). So Δx = ( (R-r)(a+R+r) )/(2a). Similarly, we have Q and y_A^2, but maybe we can also compute (t+s)/2.

Let’s compute v·v = Δx^2+Δy^2. And we have Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So v·v = Δx^2 + (y_A^2+Q)^2/(4y_A^2) = (4y_A^2Δx^2 + (y_A^2+Q)^2)/(4y_A^2). That's fine.

Now compute t and s explicitly in terms of a, r, R. We have:

t = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx) / (v·v). And s = (y_A^2+Q - 2(x_A - a)Δx) / (v·v)? Actually, s = t + 2a Δx/(v·v). So s = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx + 2a Δx)/(v·v) = (y_A^2+Q - 2Δx(x_A - a))/(v·v). And x_A - a = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) - a = (a^2+r^2-R^2-2a^2)/(2a) = (-a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = -(a^2+R^2-r^2)/(2a). So (x_A - a)Δx = -(a^2+R^2-r^2)/(2a) * Δx. That may be expressed in terms of something.

But perhaps we can compute (t+s)/2:

(t+s)/2 = (2t + 2aΔx/(v·v))/2 = t + aΔx/(v·v). So (t+s)/2 = t + aΔx/(v·v). Now t = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx)/(v·v). So:

(t+s)/2 = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx + aΔx)/(v·v) = (y_A^2+Q - Δx(2x_A - a))/(v·v).

Now note that 2x_A - a = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/a - a = (a^2+r^2-R^2 - a^2)/a = (r^2-R^2)/a = -(R^2-r^2)/a. So 2x_A - a = -(R^2-r^2)/a. Then Δx(2x_A - a) = -Δx (R^2-r^2)/a.

Thus (t+s)/2 = (y_A^2+Q + Δx (R^2-r^2)/a) / (v·v).

Now we also have X - x_A = (R^2-r^2)/(2a). So we can express some things.

Now, we need to compute the equation (1) for Y:

( X - x_A - (t+s)/2 Δx ) Δx + (Y - y_A - (t+s)/2 Δy) Δy = 0.

Let’s denote:

U = X - x_A - (t+s)/2 Δx = (R^2-r^2)/(2a) - ((t+s)/2) Δx.

Then equation is: U Δx + (Y - y_A - (t+s)/2 Δy) Δy = 0.

So Y - y_A - (t+s)/2 Δy = - U Δx / Δy.

Thus Y = y_A + (t+s)/2 Δy - U Δx / Δy.

But that might be messy. Instead, we can compute Y directly from the condition that O is equidistant from B and E, which gave Eq1. But we also have that O is on x = a/2. So we can plug X = a/2 into Eq1, and then solve for Y. Let's do that.

Eq1: 2Y y_A = -tΔx(X - x_A) - tΔy(Y - y_A) + (t^2/2) v·v.

But we have X - x_A = (R^2-r^2)/(2a). Also, Δy = y_P - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). And v·v = Δx^2+Δy^2.

So Eq1 becomes:

2Y y_A = -tΔx * ((R^2-r^2)/(2a)) - tΔy(Y - y_A) + (t^2/2) v·v.

We can try to solve for Y. This is linear in Y. Rearranged:

2Y y_A + tΔy(Y - y_A) = -tΔx ((R^2-r^2)/(2a)) + (t^2/2) v·v.

=> 2Y y_A + tΔy Y - tΔy y_A = -tΔx ((R^2-r^2)/(2a)) + (t^2/2) v·v.

=> Y (2y_A + tΔy) = tΔy y_A - tΔx ((R^2-r^2)/(2a)) + (t^2/2) v·v.

Thus Y = [ tΔy y_A - tΔx ((R^2-r^2)/(2a)) + (t^2/2) v·v ] / (2y_A + tΔy).   (EqY)

Now we also have expression for t and v·v in terms of a, r, R. This may simplify to something like Y = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A) * something? But maybe we can also compute Y from the perpendicular bisector condition, and then show that the distance from O to L equals radius.

Alternatively, we can try to compute the radius OB^2 directly and then compute the distance from O to line L and compare. That might be simpler: Compute OB^2 = (a/2 - x_A)^2 + (Y + y_A)^2. We have a/2 - x_A = (R^2-r^2)/(2a). So that term is (R^2-r^2)^2/(4a^2). And (Y+y_A)^2 will involve Y. If we can find Y explicitly, we can compute OB^2. Also, compute distance from O to line L. Line L: through H = (x_P, h_y) with direction v. So its equation can be written as: (x - x_P, y - h_y) × v = 0 (cross product). The distance from O to L is |(O - H) × v| / |v|. So we need to show that |(O-H) × v|^2 / |v|^2 = OB^2.

Thus we need to prove:

|(O-H) × v|^2 = OB^2 |v|^2.

But O and H are known in terms of a, r, R. This is an algebraic identity that we can attempt to verify.

Given the complexity, we might try to find a synthetic proof that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to (BEF). Possibly by showing that H is the center of a circle that is orthogonal to (BEF) or something.

Let's try to explore geometric properties.

We have circles Ω and Γ intersect at A and B. Points C and D are on line MN such that C is on Ω opposite N, and D on Γ beyond N. P is circumcenter of ACD. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D.

Now consider the homothety with center A that sends Ω to Γ? Actually, there is a spiral similarity (center at A) that sends Ω to Γ because A is an intersection point. Indeed, the spiral similarity centered at A that sends M to N also sends the circle Ω to Γ. Since A is on both circles, the rotation+scaling at A sending point M to N will map Ω to Γ. This spiral similarity also sends point C (on Ω) to point D? Not necessarily, because C is on Ω but not necessarily mapped to D under that spiral similarity. However, the spiral similarity that sends Ω to Γ is the composition of a rotation and scaling that sends M to N and also sends the direction of the radius at A? Actually, for two circles intersecting at A and B, there are two spiral similarities: one centered at A sending one circle to the other, and one centered at B. Under the spiral similarity centered at A, points on Ω map to points on Γ. In particular, the point C on Ω maps to some point on Γ that lies on line through A and C? Since spiral similarity at A sends any point X to X' such that triangle A X X' is similar to triangle A M N? Actually, the spiral similarity with center A that sends M to N also sends any point X on Ω to the intersection of line through A and X with Γ? Let's check: The spiral similarity at A sends M to N, so it sends the ray AM to AN. Since A is on both circles, the spiral similarity sends the circle Ω (which is centered at M) to the circle Γ (centered at N). Under this transformation, a point X on Ω maps to a point X' on Γ such that A, X, X' are collinear? Not necessarily. For a spiral similarity (rotation+scaling) about A, the image of a point X is obtained by rotating AX by some angle θ and scaling by factor k. So the image X' lies on the ray from A that is rotated from AX by θ. So generally, X' is not on line AX unless θ=0. So the spiral similarity that sends M to N does not necessarily send C to D along line AC. So that may not be directly.

But there is a known fact: The circle through A, C, D has center P. And we have that M and N are inside that circle. The orthocenter H of triangle PMN lies on the vertical line through P. There might be a relation with the circle through B, E, F.

Let's try to find a synthetic proof using inversion about A. Under inversion with center A and arbitrary radius, the circles Ω and Γ become lines ℓ1 and ℓ2 intersecting at B'. The line AP becomes itself (since through A). Points E and F become points E' and F' on ℓ1 and ℓ2 respectively, and also on AP (since AP maps to itself). So E' = AP ∩ ℓ1, F' = AP ∩ ℓ2. And B' = ℓ1 ∩ ℓ2.

Now triangle BEF: Under inversion, the circumcircle of BEF becomes some circle (or line) through A? Since B is on the circles, its image B' is intersection of lines. And E, F map to E', F' on AP. So triangle BEF inverts to triangle B' E' F'? Actually, inversion maps points to points, but not necessarily preserving collinearity or circles. The circumcircle of BEF, which passes through B, E, F, will invert to a circle passing through B', E', F' (since inversion maps circles not through A to circles not through A, and if the circle passes through A, it maps to a line). Does the circumcircle of BEF pass through A? Not generally, as we saw in numeric. So its image is a circle through B', E', F'. So we have a circle through B', E', F'. The line through H parallel to AP: Under inversion, this line (which does not pass through A generally) maps to a circle through A. That circle will intersect the circle (B'E'F') at the images of H and something. But we need tangency condition: The line L (through H parallel to AP) is tangent to circle (BEF). Under inversion, tangency is preserved if the line does not pass through A (since inversion maps circles to circles and lines to circles, but tangency is preserved if we consider oriented circles). However, the image of L under inversion is a circle through A, and the image of circle (BEF) is some circle through B', E', F'. The tangency condition becomes that the circle through A (image of L) is tangent to the circle through B', E', F' at the image of the point of tangency? Actually, if a line L is tangent to a circle (BEF) at point T, then under inversion, the images of L and (BEF) will intersect at the image of T, and they will be tangent at that point. So we can transform the problem into proving that the circle through A (image of L) is tangent to the circle through B', E', F' at the image of T. But T is the point of tangency of L with (BEF). Under inversion, T maps to some point T' on both images. So we need to show that the circle (A-image of L) and circle (B'E'F') are tangent. However, this may be more complicated.

Given the algebra is manageable, we can produce a proof by coordinates. We just need to set up and simplify, using the derived expressions. Let's attempt to compute O and then the distance condition.

We have:

a = MN = d.
Define:
x_A = (a^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2a)
y_A = √(r^2 - x_A^2) > 0.
Define:
Δ = (a+R+r)/2
x_P = (a+R-r)/2
Δx = x_P - x_A = ((R-r)Δ)/a.

Now compute y_P. Use equation: PA^2 = PC^2.

We have:
(x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.

=> (x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 + (y_P^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2) = y_P^2.

=> (x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = 0.

=> 2y_P y_A = (x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 + y_A^2.

Now compute (x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 = ( (x_P - x_A) - (x_P + r) ) ( (x_P - x_A) + (x_P + r) ) = ( -x_A - r ) ( 2x_P + r - x_A ) = -(x_A + r)(2x_P + r - x_A). So:

2y_P y_A = -(x_A + r)(2x_P + r - x_A) + y_A^2.

Thus y_P = [ y_A^2 - (x_A + r)(2x_P + r - x_A) ]/(2y_A).

Now we also have x_P = (a+R-r)/2, so 2x_P + r - x_A = (a+R-r) + r - x_A = a + R - x_A. That's nice! Because 2x_P + r = a+R-r+r = a+R, so 2x_P + r - x_A = a+R - x_A. And we also have x_A+r. So:

y_P = [ y_A^2 - (x_A + r)(a+R - x_A) ]/(2y_A) = [ y_A^2 - Q ]/(2y_A), where Q = (x_A+r)(a+R-x_A). This matches earlier: y_P = y_A/2 - Q/(2y_A). Good.

Now compute Δy = y_P - y_A = [ y_A^2 - Q ]/(2y_A) - y_A = (y_A^2 - Q - 2y_A^2)/(2y_A) = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A).

Now v = (Δx, Δy). Compute v·v = Δx^2 + (y_A^2+Q)^2/(4y_A^2).

Now we need t = s_E. Using formula: t = -2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) / (v·v). Compute x_A Δx + y_A Δy = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2.

Thus t = -2 (x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2) / (v·v) = ( -2x_A Δx + (y_A^2+Q) ) / (v·v) = ( y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx ) / (v·v).

Now note that 2x_A Δx = 2x_A * ((R-r)Δ)/a. And we have expressions for x_A and Δ.

Similarly, s = t + 2a Δx/(v·v). So s = ( y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx + 2a Δx )/(v·v) = ( y_A^2+Q - 2Δx(x_A - a) )/(v·v).

Now, we want to find the circumcenter O of B, E, F. We suspect that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of MN, which is x = a/2. Let's try to prove that by showing that the perpendicular bisector of EF (which is a line perpendicular to v) passes through the point with x = a/2? Actually, the center O is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of EF and BE. We can compute the equation of perpendicular bisector of EF: it is line through M_EF = A + (t+s)/2 v, perpendicular to v. So its equation: (X - x_A - (t+s)/2 Δx)Δx + (Y - y_A - (t+s)/2 Δy)Δy = 0. We want to see if the point (a/2, Y) satisfies this for some Y. That gives a linear equation for Y. But we also need the condition that OB^2 = OE^2, which will determine Y uniquely. So if we can show that for X = a/2, there is a Y that satisfies both, then O lies on x = a/2. That is plausible.

Let's compute the coordinates of O directly using the condition that O is equidistant from B and E, and also from B and F. Instead of solving for X and Y, we can use the fact that the circle through B, E, F is the circle with diameter? That would be symmetric.

Given the algebraic verification is straightforward, we can present a coordinate proof. The key is to choose a coordinate system that simplifies the problem. We already set M=(0,0), N=(d,0) with d>0. Then C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). Then compute P, H, E, F, B, then find circumcircle of BEF, then show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent. We can do this by showing that the distance from the center of (BEF) to the line equals the radius. We need to compute the center of (BEF) explicitly. Let's try to compute O = (X, Y) using the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BE. Perhaps we can find that X = d/2 and Y = - (y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A) * something? Actually, from the numeric, O's y-coordinate was -1.739, and y_A=0.9682, Q=1.75*3.75? Actually, for the first example, y_A^2+Q = 0.9375+3.75=4.6875, so (y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A)=4.6875/(1.9365)=2.422. Not -1.739. So Y is not that.

Maybe we can compute O by solving the equations directly using the expressions for t and s. Let's attempt to find O in terms of a, r, R.

We have B = (x_A, -y_A). E = A + t v = (x_A + t Δx, y_A + t Δy). F = A + s v = (x_A + s Δx, y_A + s Δy). Since v = (Δx, Δy), and t, s are known.

We can use the fact that the circumcenter O of triangle BEF is the point where the perpendicular bisectors of EF and BE meet. The perpendicular bisector of EF is line through M_EF = (x_A + (t+s)/2 Δx, y_A + (t+s)/2 Δy) and perpendicular to v. So its equation: (X - x_A - (t+s)/2 Δx)Δx + (Y - y_A - (t+s)/2 Δy)Δy = 0. This is a line.

The perpendicular bisector of BE: we need to find its equation. Points B and E. Midpoint of BE: ((x_A + x_A + t Δx)/2, (-y_A + y_A + t Δy)/2) = (x_A + (t Δx)/2, (t Δy)/2). The slope of BE: (y_A + t Δy - (-y_A)) / (x_A + t Δx - x_A) = (2y_A + t Δy) / (t Δx). So the perpendicular slope is - (t Δx) / (2y_A + t Δy). The equation of perpendicular bisector: (Y - (t Δy)/2) = - (t Δx) / (2y_A + t Δy) * (X - x_A - (t Δx)/2). This is a line.

Now we want the intersection O of these two lines. We can solve for X and Y. Since we suspect X = a/2, we can plug X = a/2 into the perpendicular bisector of BE and solve for Y. Then check that this Y also satisfies the perpendicular bisector of EF. That will give a condition that must hold, which we can verify using the expressions.

Alternatively, we can find O directly by solving the linear equations. Let's try to compute O using the condition that O is equidistant from B and E, and also from B and F. That gives two equations:

(1) (X - x_A)^2 + (Y + y_A)^2 = (X - x_A - t Δx)^2 + (Y - y_A - t Δy)^2.
(2) (X - x_A)^2 + (Y + y_A)^2 = (X - x_A - s Δx)^2 + (Y - y_A - s Δy)^2.

These are linear in X and Y after expansion? Actually, they become:

(1) Expand: (X-x_A)^2 + (Y+y_A)^2 = (X-x_A)^2 - 2tΔx(X-x_A) + t^2Δx^2 + (Y-y_A)^2 - 2tΔy(Y-y_A) + t^2Δy^2.
Cancel (X-x_A)^2:
(Y+y_A)^2 = -2tΔx(X-x_A) + t^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + (Y-y_A)^2 - 2tΔy(Y-y_A).
Now (Y+y_A)^2 - (Y-y_A)^2 = 4Y y_A. So:
4Y y_A = -2tΔx(X-x_A) - 2tΔy(Y-y_A) + t^2 v·v.
=> 2Y y_A = -tΔx(X-x_A) - tΔy(Y-y_A) + (t^2/2) v·v.   (1)

Similarly, (2) gives:
2Y y_A = -sΔx(X-x_A) - sΔy(Y-y_A) + (s^2/2) v·v.   (2)

Now these are linear equations in X and Y? Actually, they are linear in X and Y because we have terms like X and Y on the RHS. We can rearrange:

(1): 2Y y_A + tΔy(Y - y_A) = -tΔx(X-x_A) + (t^2/2) v·v.
=> 2Y y_A + tΔy Y - tΔy y_A = -tΔx X + tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v.
=> Y (2y_A + tΔy) = -tΔx X + tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A.
=> Y = [ -tΔx X + tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A ] / (2y_A + tΔy).   (1')

Similarly, (2): Y = [ -sΔx X + sΔx x_A + (s^2/2) v·v + sΔy y_A ] / (2y_A + sΔy).   (2')

We can solve for X and Y by equating these. This might be messy but perhaps we can use the fact that t and s satisfy s = t + 2aΔx/(v·v) and also that v·v = Δx^2+Δy^2.

Alternatively, we can try to find X from the difference of the two equations. Subtract (1) from (2):

2Y y_A = -sΔx(X-x_A) - sΔy(Y-y_A) + (s^2/2)v·v
2Y y_A = -tΔx(X-x_A) - tΔy(Y-y_A) + (t^2/2)v·v

Subtract: 0 = -(s-t)Δx(X-x_A) - (s-t)Δy(Y-y_A) + ((s^2 - t^2)/2)v·v.
=> (s-t)Δx(X-x_A) + (s-t)Δy(Y-y_A) = ((s-t)(s+t)/2) v·v.
Since s≠t, divide by (s-t): Δx(X-x_A) + Δy(Y-y_A) = ((s+t)/2) v·v.   (A)

But note that Δx(X-x_A) + Δy(Y-y_A) = (X,Y) dot v - (x_A,y_A) dot v. This is a linear equation in X and Y.

Now, we also have that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF. That gave: (X - x_A - (t+s)/2 Δx)Δx + (Y - y_A - (t+s)/2 Δy)Δy = 0.
=> Δx X + Δy Y = Δx x_A + Δy y_A + ((t+s)/2)(Δx^2+Δy^2) = (x_A,y_A)·v + ((t+s)/2) v·v.   (B)

Now (A) gives: Δx X + Δy Y = (x_A,y_A)·v + ((s+t)/2) v·v. So (A) and (B) are exactly the same equation! So the condition that O is equidistant from B and E (or F) actually gives that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF. So the two conditions are not independent; we need to use both OB=OE and OB=OF to determine O. Actually, we used OB=OE and OB=OF; they gave two equations that are not equivalent. But from subtracting, we got a relation that is essentially the perpendicular bisector of EF. So the perpendicular bisector of EF is one of the conditions that O must satisfy. So O is the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BE (or BF). So we can use that.

Thus we have:

(1) Δx X + Δy Y = (x_A,y_A)·v + ((t+s)/2) v·v.   (Equation B)

(2) From OB=OE, we have Eq1: 2Y y_A = -tΔx(X-x_A) - tΔy(Y-y_A) + (t^2/2) v·v. That's another linear equation in X and Y? Actually, it involves X and Y, and we can write it as:

2Y y_A + tΔy(Y - y_A) = -tΔx(X-x_A) + (t^2/2) v·v.
=> 2Y y_A + tΔy Y - tΔy y_A = -tΔx X + tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v.
=> Y (2y_A + tΔy) + tΔx X = tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A.   (Eq1')

So we have a system of two linear equations in X and Y: (B) and (Eq1').

Now we can solve for X and Y. Since we suspect X = a/2, we can try to see if that solution satisfies both. So we can plug X = a/2 into (B) and solve for Y, then check that the resulting Y also satisfies (Eq1'). But we can also solve directly.

Let's compute the coefficients. We have:

v = (Δx, Δy). We also have (x_A,y_A)·v = x_A Δx + y_A Δy.

We know Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). Also, we have expression for t. Let's try to compute some quantities.

We have:

v·v = Δx^2 + Δy^2.

Also, from earlier, t = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx)/(v·v).

Now compute (x_A,y_A)·v = x_A Δx + y_A Δy = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2.

Thus (x_A,y_A)·v + ((t+s)/2) v·v = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2 + ((t+s)/2) v·v.

But t+s = (2t+2aΔx/(v·v)) = 2t + 2aΔx/(v·v). So (t+s)/2 = t + aΔx/(v·v). So:

RHS = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2 + (t + aΔx/(v·v)) v·v = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2 + t v·v + aΔx.

Now t v·v = y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx. So:

RHS = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2 + (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx) + aΔx = x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2 + y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx + aΔx = (y_A^2+Q) - (y_A^2+Q)/2 - x_A Δx + aΔx = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A).

Thus equation (B) becomes:

Δx X + Δy Y = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A).   (B')

Now note that a - x_A = (R^2 - r^2)/a? Actually, we computed earlier: a - x_A = a - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (2a^2 - a^2 - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = (a^2 + R^2 - r^2)/(2a). And also we have X - x_A = (R^2 - r^2)/(2a). So a - x_A = (a^2 + R^2 - r^2)/(2a). This is not simply related to X - x_A. But we have Δx = ((R-r)Δ)/a. And we also have (y_A^2+Q)/2.

Now we also have Eq1': Y (2y_A + tΔy) + tΔx X = tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A.

We can try to solve for X and Y. Maybe we can find that X = a/2 by substituting into these equations and using the relation for Y. Alternatively, we can compute Y from (B') as Y = [ (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) - Δx X ] / Δy. Then plug into Eq1' and see if it holds identically. That would prove that the intersection point of (B') and Eq1' has X = a/2? Actually, we need to find X that satisfies both. Let's treat X as unknown and Y given by (B'). Then Eq1' becomes an equation in X. We can try to solve for X.

From (B'): Y = [ (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) - Δx X ] / Δy.

Plug into Eq1'. This will be linear in X. So we can solve for X and see if it simplifies to a/2. That is a viable algebraic verification.

Given the heavy algebra, we can attempt to compute X symbolically. We have:

Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So 1/Δy = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q).

Thus Y = [ (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) - Δx X ] * (-2y_A/(y_A^2+Q)) = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * [ (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) - Δx X ] = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * [ (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) - Δx X ].

Simplify: = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * ( (y_A^2+Q)/2 ) - 2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * Δx (a - x_A) + 2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * Δx X? Wait, careful:

Y = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * ( (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) - Δx X ) = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * ( (y_A^2+Q)/2 ) -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * Δx (a - x_A) + 2y_A/(y_A^2+Q) * Δx X = -y_A - (2y_A Δx (a - x_A))/(y_A^2+Q) + (2y_A Δx X)/(y_A^2+Q).

Thus Y = -y_A + (2y_A Δx)/(y_A^2+Q) (X - (a - x_A)).   (Y_expr)

Now plug into Eq1': Y (2y_A + tΔy) + tΔx X = tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A.

But we can also compute (2y_A + tΔy). Since Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A), we have tΔy = -t(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So:

2y_A + tΔy = 2y_A - t(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A) = (4y_A^2 - t(y_A^2+Q))/(2y_A). We'll keep it.

Now substitute Y from Y_expr. This will be an equation in X. This is heavy but we can try to simplify using the expression for t and v·v.

Alternatively, we can try to compute X directly by solving the system of two linear equations in X and Y: (B') and Eq1'. We can use the method of determinants.

Let's write (B') as:

Δx X + Δy Y = C, where C = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A).   (1)

Eq1' is: tΔx X + (2y_A + tΔy) Y = D, where D = tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A.   (2)

We can solve for X:

X = (C (2y_A + tΔy) - Δy D) / (Δx (2y_A + tΔy) - tΔx Δy). Actually, using Cramer's rule:

X = det( [C, Δy; D, (2y_A + tΔy)] ) / det( [Δx, Δy; tΔx, (2y_A + tΔy)] ) = (C(2y_A + tΔy) - Δy D) / (Δx(2y_A + tΔy) - tΔx Δy). Similarly, Y = (Δx D - tΔx C) / (Δx(2y_A + tΔy) - tΔx Δy).

We can attempt to compute these and show that X = a/2. This may be simplified by substituting the known expressions for t, Δy, v·v, etc. However, it's still heavy.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, there should be a synthetic solution that is not too heavy. Let's try to find one.

Maybe we can use the fact that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N are centers, and P is circumcenter of ACD, we can try to relate AP to something like the radical axis of two circles.

Consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Let's denote O_BEF = O. Then we want line L through H parallel to AP to be tangent to (BEF). That means the distance from O to L equals radius R_BEF. Equivalently, the power of H with respect to (BEF) is equal to the square of the distance from H to O minus R^2? No.

But perhaps we can show that H is the center of a spiral similarity that sends the circle (BEF) to the line L. That would mean that L is the polar of H with respect to (BEF) if H is on the circle? Not.

Alternatively, we can try to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of H with respect to triangle BEF? For a point H on the circumcircle of triangle BEF, the Simson line is the line through the feet of perpendiculars from H to sides. That line is tangent to the nine-point circle, not directly.

But maybe H is the orthocenter of triangle BEF? Then the line through H parallel to AP would be the line through orthocenter parallel to something; not.

Let's compute for numeric: triangle BEF, orthocenter? B(-0.25,-0.9682), E(0.875,-0.4828), F(2,-1.9345). Find orthocenter: altitude from B to EF? EF is along v=(1.5,-1.9351). So altitude from B is perpendicular to v. Slope of v = -1.9351/1.5=-1.29, so perpendicular slope = 1/1.29=0.775. Equation through B: y+0.9682 = 0.775(x+0.25). Altitude from E to BF: BF slope = (-1.9345+0.9682)/(2+0.875)=(-0.9663)/2.875=-0.336, perpendicular slope = 1/0.336=2.976. Equation through E: y+0.4828 = 2.976(x-0.875). Solve? Intersection? Could be H'? But we have H=(1.25,-0.3235). So not orthocenter.

Maybe we can find that H is the center of the circle through A, C, D? No, P is.

Given the difficulty, perhaps a coordinate proof is acceptable. The problem says "Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." In many olympiad problems, coordinate geometry is used if it leads to manageable algebra. So we can produce a proof using analytic geometry. We need to be careful to present it clearly, with proper justifications.

Thus I'll construct a coordinate proof.

Set up:

Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d>0. Let r = OM, R = ON, with r < R. The circles intersect, so |R-r| < d < R+r. Without loss, assume A is the intersection point with positive y-coordinate, so A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A > 0. Then B = (x_A, -y_A). The line MN is the x-axis. The points C and D are defined as the intersections of line MN with Ω and Γ, respectively, such that C, M, N, D lie in that order. So C is on Ω on the opposite side of M from N, so C = (-r, 0). D is on Γ on the ray from N away from M, so D = (d+R, 0). (We can verify that order: -r < 0 < d < d+R.)

Now compute P, the circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D lie on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line through midpoint of C and D. Midpoint: ((-r + d+R)/2, 0) = (x_P, 0) where x_P = (d+R-r)/2. So P = (x_P, y_P) for some y_P. From equal distances: PA = PC. So:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2.   (1)

We can solve for y_P. Expand:

(x_P - x_A)^2 + y_P^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = (x_P + r)^2 + y_P^2
=> (x_P - x_A)^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = (x_P + r)^2
=> 2y_P y_A = (x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 + y_A^2.

Now compute (x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 = ((x_P - x_A) - (x_P + r))((x_P - x_A)+(x_P + r)) = (-x_A - r)(2x_P + r - x_A). But note that x_P = (d+R-r)/2, so 2x_P + r = d+R. Thus 2x_P + r - x_A = d+R - x_A. So:

(x_P - x_A)^2 - (x_P + r)^2 = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A). Therefore:

2y_P y_A = -(x_A + r)(d+R - x_A) + y_A^2.
=> y_P = (y_A^2 - (x_A + r)(d+R - x_A))/(2y_A).   (2)

Let Q = (x_A + r)(d+R - x_A). Then y_P = (y_A^2 - Q)/(2y_A). Also note that Q = (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A). Using the circle equations, we can compute y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2, and also we have relation: (x_A - d)^2 + y_A^2 = R^2 => x_A^2 - 2d x_A + d^2 + y_A^2 = R^2 => (x_A^2+y_A^2) -2d x_A + d^2 = R^2 => r^2 - 2d x_A + d^2 = R^2 => 2d x_A = d^2 + r^2 - R^2 => x_A = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d). Good.

Now compute the vector v = AP = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). From (2), y_P - y_A = (y_A^2 - Q)/(2y_A) - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So:

v = (Δx, Δy) with Δx = x_P - x_A = ((d+R-r)/2) - ((d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d)) = ((d+R-r)/2) - (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d). We can simplify: Δx = ((R-r)(d+R+r))/(2d). Indeed, we derived earlier. So:

Δx = ((R-r)(d+R+r))/(2d).   (3)

Also, Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). We'll keep it.

Now consider the line AP. It meets Ω again at E, and Γ again at F. Parameterize: A + s v, s real. Since A is on Ω, the second intersection E corresponds to s = s_E satisfying:

(x_A + s Δx)^2 + (y_A + s Δy)^2 = r^2.
Because x_A^2+y_A^2=r^2, we get:

2s(x_A Δx + y_A Δy) + s^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) = 0.
Thus s=0 (A) and s = s_E = -2(x_A Δx + y_A Δy)/(Δx^2+Δy^2). So:

s_E = -2(x_A Δx + y_A Δy)/v·v.   (4)

Similarly, for Γ: (x-d)^2+y^2 = R^2. For point on AP: x = x_A + s Δx, y = y_A + s Δy. Since A satisfies (x_A-d)^2+y_A^2=R^2, we have:

2s((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy) + s^2 v·v = 0.
Thus s=0 (A) and s = s_F = -2((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy)/v·v. So:

s_F = -2((x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy)/v·v.   (5)

Note that (x_A-d)Δx + y_A Δy = x_A Δx + y_A Δy - d Δx. So:

s_F = s_E + 2d Δx/v·v.   (6)

Now define points:

B = (x_A, -y_A).
E = A + s_E v = (x_A + s_E Δx, y_A + s_E Δy).
F = A + s_F v = (x_A + s_F Δx, y_A + s_F Δy).

We want to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. Let H be orthocenter of triangle PMN. Coordinates: M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(x_P, y_P). The orthocenter H is the intersection of altitudes. Altitude from P to MN: since MN is horizontal, altitude is vertical line x = x_P. Altitude from M to PN: line through M perpendicular to PN. Vector PN = (d - x_P, -y_P). So direction of PN = (d-x_P, -y_P). Perpendicular slope = (d-x_P)/y_P? Actually, the line through M perpendicular to PN has direction perpendicular to PN, so slope = - (d-x_P)/y_P? Let's compute: slope of PN = -y_P/(d-x_P). Perpendicular slope = (d-x_P)/y_P. So altitude from M: y = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x. Intersection with x=x_P gives y_H = ((d-x_P)/y_P) x_P. So:

H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P).   (7)

Now line L through H parallel to AP: direction v. So its equation can be written as: (x - x_P, y - y_H) is parallel to v. Alternatively, the distance from a point O to L is |(O-H) × v|/|v|.

Now we need to find the circumcircle of BEF. Let O be its center. We claim that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of MN, i.e., on line x = d/2. We can prove that by showing that the perpendicular bisector of EF (which is a line perpendicular to v) passes through the point (d/2, Y) for some Y, and also that OB=OE, which gives the Y. But we can directly compute O by solving the linear equations from OB=OE and OB=OF. However, we can also find O by using the fact that the circle through B, E, F is the reflection of the circle through A, C, D? Not.

Given the algebra, we can compute O explicitly using the system we had. Perhaps we can find a simpler expression for O. Let's try to compute O in terms of a, r, R by solving the linear system.

We have:

(1) Δx X + Δy Y = C, where C = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A). (from B')
(2) tΔx X + (2y_A + tΔy) Y = D, where D = tΔx x_A + (t^2/2) v·v + tΔy y_A.

We can compute t and other quantities in terms of a, r, R. Let's attempt to simplify these expressions.

We have a = d.

Compute x_A = (a^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2a).
y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2 = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (a-r)^2)/(4a^2). But we may not need explicit y_A.

Compute Q = (x_A + r)(a+R - x_A). We already have Q = (a^2 - (R-r)^2)(a+R+r)^2/(4a^2). And y_A^2 = ((r+R)^2 - a^2)((a+R)^2 - r^2)/(4a^2).

Now compute y_A^2 + Q. We derived earlier that y_A^2+Q = ( (r+R)^2 - a^2)((a+R)^2 - r^2) + (a^2 - (R-r)^2)(a+R+r)^2 all over 4a^2. That might simplify. Let's compute y_A^2+Q in a more symmetric way.

Let’s denote:
u = a+R+r, v = a+R-r? Actually, better: Define S = a+R+r, T = a+R-r, U = a+r+R? That's the same as S. Not.

We have:
y_A^2 = ((r+R)^2 - a^2)((a+R)^2 - r^2)/(4a^2).
Q = (a^2 - (R-r)^2)(a+R+r)^2/(4a^2).

So y_A^2+Q = [ ((r+R)^2 - a^2)((a+R)^2 - r^2) + (a^2 - (R-r)^2)(a+R+r)^2 ]/(4a^2).

Now note that ((r+R)^2 - a^2) = (r+R-a)(r+R+a). And (a+R)^2 - r^2 = (a+R-r)(a+R+r). So first product = (r+R-a)(r+R+a)(a+R-r)(a+R+r) = (r+R-a)(a+R-r) * (r+R+a)(a+R+r). And (r+R-a)(a+R-r) =? Expand: (r+R-a)(a+R-r) = ( (R+r)-a )( (R+r)-a? Actually, compute: (r+R-a)(a+R-r) = ( (R+r)-a )( (R+r)-a )? Wait, (r+R-a) = (R+r-a). (a+R-r) = (R+a-r). Not the same. But product = (R+r-a)(R+a-r) =? Let's compute: (R+r-a)(R+a-r) = (R + (r-a))(R + (a-r)) = R^2 + R(a-r) + R(r-a) + (r-a)(a-r) = R^2 + R(a-r+r-a) + (r-a)(a-r) = R^2 + 0 + (r-a)(a-r) = R^2 - (a-r)^2. So (r+R-a)(a+R-r) = R^2 - (a-r)^2 = (R - (a-r))(R + (a-r)) = (R - a + r)(R + a - r). That is not obviously equal to (a^2 - (R-r)^2)? Actually, (a^2 - (R-r)^2) = (a - (R-r))(a + (R-r)) = (a - R + r)(a + R - r) = (r+a-R)(a+R-r). So (a^2 - (R-r)^2) = (r+a-R)(a+R-r). That's the product we have. So (r+R-a)(a+R-r) =? We computed as R^2 - (a-r)^2. Compare to (a^2 - (R-r)^2) = (a-R+r)(a+R-r) = (r+a-R)(a+R-r). So indeed (a^2 - (R-r)^2) = (r+a-R)(a+R-r). And (r+R-a)(a+R-r) = (R+r-a)(a+R-r) = (R+r-a)(a+R-r). This is different. So maybe we can simplify the sum.

Let's compute numerically for the first example to see if y_A^2+Q is something like (a+R+r)^2 * something? For a=1.5, r=1, R=2: y_A^2+Q = 0.9375+3.75=4.6875. (a+R+r)^2 = (1.5+2+1)^2=4.5^2=20.25. So not.

But we might not need explicit y_A^2+Q if we use the derived expressions for t and C.

From earlier, we have:

t = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx)/(v·v).
Also, C = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A).

We also have v·v = Δx^2+Δy^2.

Now, we also have Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So 1/Δy = -2y_A/(y_A^2+Q).

Thus Y_expr = -y_A + (2y_A Δx)/(y_A^2+Q) (X - (a - x_A)). That came from (B').

Now, we also have Eq1' (from OB=OE). But maybe we can use a different approach: Instead of solving for O directly, we can compute the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) and show that it equals the square of the distance from H to the circle's center minus radius squared? That is not simpler.

Alternatively, we can find the equation of circle (BEF) and then show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent by verifying that the equation of the line and the circle have a double root. That is, if we param the line as H + t v, then we need to show that the quadratic in t (from substituting into circle equation) has discriminant zero. That might be easier: The circle (BEF) is defined by points B, E, F. We can compute its equation in terms of coordinates. Then substitute the line param and show that the quadratic has a double root. But that still requires the circle equation.

Given the complexity, we can attempt to compute the circle (BEF) explicitly using the fact that E and F are on line AP, and B is symmetric to A. Perhaps there is a known circle: The circle through B, E, F is the reflection of the circle through A, C, D across the line AB? Not.

Let's try to find a synthetic proof using properties of orthocenters and cyclic quadrilaterals.

We have triangle PMN. Its orthocenter H. Since M and N are on x-axis, the altitude from P is vertical, so H lies on that vertical line. Also, the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN. So we have that H is the circumcenter of the triangle formed by the reflections? Actually, in any triangle, the reflection of the orthocenter across a side lies on the circumcircle. So reflect H across MN: call it H'. Since MN is horizontal, H' = (x_P, -y_H). And H' lies on the circumcircle of triangle PMN. Also, note that P is the circumcenter of ACD. So maybe H' is related to A, C, D.

Consider the circumcircle of triangle PMN. Its center is the midpoint of the segment joining the circumcenter and orthocenter? Actually, the nine-point circle has center at the midpoint of OH. But not.

Another idea: Use vector geometry. Let’s denote vectors with origin at M? Since M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Then we have:

M = 0, N = d (vector along x-axis).
P = (x_P, y_P).
We have orthocenter H = (x_P, x_P(d-x_P)/y_P). So H = (x_P, (x_P(d-x_P))/y_P).

Now line L through H with direction v. We need to prove L is tangent to circle (BEF). So if we can find the circle (BEF) and show that the distance from its center to L equals radius, we are done.

Let's compute the center O of circle (BEF). We have B = (x_A, -y_A). E = A + s_E v. F = A + s_F v. Since v = (Δx, Δy), we can compute O by using the fact that the circle through B and the two points on line AP is the circle with diameter? Maybe we can find O as the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of EF (which is line through M_EF perpendicular to v) and the perpendicular bisector of BE. That is a linear system. We can try to find O by using the fact that the circle (BEF) is symmetric to circle (AEF) across MN? Because B is reflection of A across MN, and E, F are on AP. But AP is not symmetric across MN; its reflection is line through B and P' (reflection of P). So not.

However, note that if we reflect the entire configuration across MN, then Ω and Γ are invariant, A and B swap, P becomes P' (reflection of P), and the line AP becomes line B P'. The points E and F, being on AP, reflect to points E' and F' on B P'. The circle (BEF) reflects to circle (B E' F')? Actually, B is fixed, so the reflected triangle B E' F' is the reflection of triangle BEF? No, triangle BEF reflects to triangle B E' F' (since B fixed, E and F reflect to E' and F'). So the circumcircle of BEF reflects to circumcircle of B E' F'. So the center O of (BEF) reflects to O' of (B E' F'), which is the reflection of O across MN. So O' = (x_O, -y_O). So if we can show that O lies on x = d/2, then O' also lies on x = d/2. That is consistent.

Now, maybe we can find a relation between AP and the line through H parallel to AP. Since H is orthocenter of PMN, and P is circumcenter of ACD, maybe there is a relation that H is the reflection of the midpoint of CD across something? Not.

Given the time, I think the coordinate proof is the most straightforward. I'll proceed to outline a rigorous coordinate proof, simplifying the algebra as much as possible. I'll compute O using Cramer's rule and then compute the distance condition. I'll use symbolic simplification.

Let me define:

Let a = MN = d.
Let r = OM, R = ON, with r < R.
Define:
x_A = (a^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2a)
y_A = √(r^2 - x_A^2) > 0.
Then B = (x_A, -y_A).

Define:
Δ = a + R + r
x_P = (a + R - r)/2
Δx = x_P - x_A = ((R - r)Δ)/(2a)   (Equation A)

Now compute Q = (x_A + r)(a + R - x_A). Using the expressions, we have:
x_A + r = (a^2 + r^2 - R^2 + 2ar)/(2a) = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)/(2a) = ((a+r-R)(a+r+R))/(2a)
a+R - x_A = a+R - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (2a(a+R) - (a^2+r^2-R^2))/(2a) = (a^2+2aR - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = ((a+R)^2 - r^2)/(2a) = ((a+R-r)(a+R+r))/(2a)
Thus Q = ((a+r-R)(a+r+R)(a+R-r)(a+R+r))/(4a^2) = ((a+r-R)(a+R-r)) * ((a+r+R)(a+R+r))/(4a^2) = ( (a^2 - (R-r)^2) * (a+R+r)^2 )/(4a^2)   (since (a+r-R)(a+R-r) = (a+(r-R))(a+(R-r)) = a^2 - (r-R)^2 = a^2 - (R-r)^2).

And also y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2 = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (a-r)^2)/(4a^2). But we may not need y_A.

Now compute Δy = y_P - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So Δy = - (y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A).

Now define v = (Δx, Δy). Compute v·v = Δx^2 + Δy^2.

Now compute s_E and s_F:

s_E = -2 (x_A Δx + y_A Δy)/v·v = -2 (x_A Δx - (y_A^2+Q)/2)/v·v = (y_A^2+Q - 2x_A Δx)/v·v.   (1)

s_F = s_E + 2a Δx/v·v.   (2)

Now define points:

E = (x_A + s_E Δx, y_A + s_E Δy)
F = (x_A + s_F Δx, y_A + s_F Δy)

Now we want the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Let's compute O by using the perpendicular bisector of EF and the perpendicular bisector of BE. The perpendicular bisector of EF: since E and F lie on line AP, the segment EF is along v. So its perpendicular bisector is line through midpoint M_EF = (x_A + (s_E+s_F)/2 Δx, y_A + (s_E+s_F)/2 Δy) and perpendicular to v. Its equation:

( X - x_A - (s_E+s_F)/2 Δx ) Δx + ( Y - y_A - (s_E+s_F)/2 Δy ) Δy = 0.   (3)

The perpendicular bisector of BE: points B and E. Midpoint of BE: ( (x_A + x_A + s_E Δx)/2, (-y_A + y_A + s_E Δy)/2 ) = ( x_A + (s_E Δx)/2, (s_E Δy)/2 ). The slope of BE: (y_A + s_E Δy - (-y_A))/(x_A + s_E Δx - x_A) = (2y_A + s_E Δy)/(s_E Δx). So the perpendicular slope is - (s_E Δx)/(2y_A + s_E Δy). So the equation of perpendicular bisector of BE:

( Y - (s_E Δy)/2 ) = - (s_E Δx)/(2y_A + s_E Δy) ( X - x_A - (s_E Δx)/2 ).   (4)

Now we need to find the intersection O of (3) and (4). We can try to solve for X and Y. Perhaps we can guess that X = a/2. Let's verify that this guess satisfies (3) for some Y, and then find Y from (3) and then check (4). So set X = a/2. Then (3) gives:

( a/2 - x_A - (s_E+s_F)/2 Δx ) Δx + ( Y - y_A - (s_E+s_F)/2 Δy ) Δy = 0.
=> Y = y_A + (s_E+s_F)/2 Δy - Δx (a/2 - x_A - (s_E+s_F)/2 Δx) / Δy.   (5)

Now we need to compute (s_E+s_F)/2. We have s_F = s_E + 2aΔx/v·v, so s_E+s_F = 2s_E + 2aΔx/v·v. Thus (s_E+s_F)/2 = s_E + aΔx/v·v.

Thus Y = y_A + (s_E + aΔx/v·v) Δy - Δx (a/2 - x_A - (s_E + aΔx/v·v) Δx) / Δy.

Now we can try to simplify using the expressions for s_E and Δy.

We also have from (4) we can plug X = a/2 and Y from (5) and then check if it holds identically. That would show that O lies on x = a/2. But we also need to find Y explicitly, and then compute the distance condition. However, we can also compute O by solving the system of two linear equations (3) and (4) directly, but that is messy.

Given that the numeric suggests O's x-coordinate is a/2, we can try to prove that O's x-coordinate is a/2 by using the fact that the perpendicular bisector of BE passes through the point (a/2, Y) for some Y that also satisfies (3). That is, we can solve for X from (3) and (4) by eliminating Y. Let's do that.

From (3): Δx X + Δy Y = x_A Δx + (s_E+s_F)/2 Δx^2 + y_A Δy + (s_E+s_F)/2 Δy^2 = (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) + ((s_E+s_F)/2)(Δx^2+Δy^2) = (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) + ((s_E+s_F)/2) v·v.   (3')

From (4): (Y - (s_E Δy)/2) = - (s_E Δx)/(2y_A + s_E Δy) (X - x_A - (s_E Δx)/2). Multiply both sides by (2y_A + s_E Δy):

(2y_A + s_E Δy) Y - (s_E Δy)(2y_A + s_E Δy)/2 = -s_E Δx (X - x_A - (s_E Δx)/2).
=> (2y_A + s_E Δy) Y + s_E Δx X = s_E Δx x_A + s_E Δx * (s_E Δx)/2 + (s_E Δy)(2y_A + s_E Δy)/2? Wait, we need to bring terms.

Let's derive properly:

Equation (4): Y - (s_E Δy)/2 = - (s_E Δx)/(2y_A + s_E Δy) ( X - x_A - (s_E Δx)/2 ).
Multiply both sides by (2y_A + s_E Δy):
(2y_A + s_E Δy)(Y - (s_E Δy)/2) = -s_E Δx (X - x_A - (s_E Δx)/2).
=> (2y_A + s_E Δy)Y - (2y_A + s_E Δy)(s_E Δy)/2 = -s_E Δx X + s_E Δx x_A + s_E Δx * (s_E Δx)/2.
=> (2y_A + s_E Δy)Y + s_E Δx X = s_E Δx x_A + s_E Δx * (s_E Δx)/2 + (2y_A + s_E Δy)(s_E Δy)/2.
Now (2y_A + s_E Δy)(s_E Δy)/2 = (s_E Δy (2y_A + s_E Δy))/2 = (2y_A s_E Δy + s_E^2 Δy^2)/2.
So RHS = s_E Δx x_A + (s_E^2 Δx^2)/2 + (2y_A s_E Δy + s_E^2 Δy^2)/2 = s_E Δx x_A + (s_E^2 (Δx^2+Δy^2))/2 + y_A s_E Δy = s_E Δx x_A + (s_E^2 v·v)/2 + y_A s_E Δy.
Thus equation (4) becomes:

s_E Δx X + (2y_A + s_E Δy) Y = s_E Δx x_A + (s_E^2 v·v)/2 + y_A s_E Δy.   (4')

Now we have (3') and (4'). We can solve for X and Y.

Let’s denote:

(3') : Δx X + Δy Y = C, where C = (x_A Δx + y_A Δy) + ((s_E+s_F)/2) v·v.
(4') : s_E Δx X + (2y_A + s_E Δy) Y = D, where D = s_E Δx x_A + (s_E^2 v·v)/2 + y_A s_E Δy.

Now we can use Cramer's rule. But we can also try to see if X = a/2 solves these. Substitute X = a/2 into (3') and (4') and see if we can find Y that satisfies both, and then check that the Y from (3') also satisfies (4'). That would prove that the intersection point has X = a/2. So we need to compute Y from (3') when X=a/2: Y = (C - Δx * a/2)/Δy. Then we need to verify that this Y also satisfies (4') when X=a/2, i.e., that:

s_E Δx * (a/2) + (2y_A + s_E Δy) * Y = D.

Plug Y from (3'): Y = (C - (Δx a)/2)/Δy. Then left side becomes:

s_E Δx * a/2 + (2y_A + s_E Δy) * (C - (Δx a)/2)/Δy = s_E Δx a/2 + (2y_A + s_E Δy) * C/Δy - (2y_A + s_E Δy)*(Δx a)/(2Δy).

We need to show this equals D.

Thus we need to prove:

s_E Δx a/2 + (2y_A + s_E Δy) C/Δy - (2y_A + s_E Δy) (Δx a)/(2Δy) = D.

Multiply both sides by 2Δy to eliminate denominators:

s_E Δx a Δy + 2(2y_A + s_E Δy) C - (2y_A + s_E Δy) Δx a = 2Δy D.   (6)

Now we can substitute the expressions for C, D, s_E, etc. This looks heavy but maybe we can simplify using the known relations.

Given the time, I think a direct coordinate proof using computer algebra is acceptable, but for a human-readable solution, we need to present a clean algebraic verification. We can find the equation of the circle by using the general equation: X^2+Y^2 + AX + BY + C = 0. Since B, E, F satisfy it, we can solve for A, B, C. But that still requires coordinates.

Given the complexity, I'll attempt to produce a synthetic proof that is known or can be derived. Let's search memory: This problem might be from an IMO Shortlist or something. The configuration: Two circles intersect, line through centers meets circles at C and D. P is circumcenter of ACD. AP meets circles again at E and F. H is orthocenter of PMN. Given the time, I think the coordinate proof is the most straightforward. I will present a solution that uses analytic geometry with careful algebraic simplification, using the derived expressions. I'll try to simplify as much as possible.

Let me attempt to compute O explicitly in a manageable way.

We have:

a = MN = d.

Define:
α = x_A = (a^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2a)
β = y_A = √(r^2 - α^2) > 0.
B = (α, -β).

Compute Δx = ((R - r)(a+R+r))/(2a) = ((R - r)u)/(2a) where u = a+R+r.
Also, x_P = (a+R-r)/2 = (a+R-r)/2.
Then Δx = x_P - α = (a+R-r)/2 - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = ( a(a+R-r) - (a^2+r^2-R^2) )/(2a) = ( a^2 + aR - ar - a^2 - r^2 + R^2 )/(2a) = ( aR - ar - r^2 + R^2 )/(2a) = ( (R-r)a + (R^2 - r^2) )/(2a) = ( (R-r)(a+R+r) )/(2a) = ((R-r)u)/(2a). Good.

Now compute Q = (α+r)(a+R-α). We have α+r = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) + r = (a^2+r^2-R^2+2ar)/(2a) = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)/(2a) = ((a+r-R)(a+r+R))/(2a) = ((a+r-R)u)/(2a). And a+R-α = a+R - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (2a(a+R) - (a^2+r^2-R^2))/(2a) = (a^2+2aR - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = ((a+R)^2 - r^2)/(2a) = ((a+R-r)(a+R+r))/(2a) = ((a+R-r)u)/(2a). Thus Q = ((a+r-R)u)/(2a) * ((a+R-r)u)/(2a) = ( (a+r-R)(a+R-r) u^2 )/(4a^2). But note that (a+r-R)(a+R-r) = (a+(r-R))(a+(R-r)) = a^2 - (R-r)^2 = a^2 - (R-r)^2. So Q = (a^2 - (R-r)^2) u^2/(4a^2).

Now y_A^2 = β^2 = r^2 - α^2 = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (a-r)^2)/(4a^2) = ( (a+r-R)(a+r+R) ) * ( (R-(a-r))(R+(a-r)) )/(4a^2) = ( (a+r-R)u ) * ( (R-a+r)(R+a-r) )/(4a^2). But R-a+r = r+R-a, and R+a-r = a+R-r. So y_A^2 = ( (a+r-R)(r+R-a) u (a+R-r) )/(4a^2). That's messy. However, note that (a+r-R)(r+R-a) = ( (r+R)-a )((r+R)-a) )? Actually, (a+r-R)(r+R-a) = ( (r+R)-a )((r+R)-a) )? Because a+r-R = r+R-a, and r+R-a = r+R-a. So they are equal: (a+r-R) = (r+R-a). So (a+r-R)(r+R-a) = (r+R-a)^2. So y_A^2 = ( (r+R-a)^2 u (a+R-r) )/(4a^2). But (a+R-r) is not necessarily equal to (r+R-a). So:

y_A^2 = ( (r+R-a)^2 (a+R-r) u )/(4a^2). Good.

Now compute y_A^2+Q = [ (r+R-a)^2 (a+R-r) u ]/(4a^2) + [ (a^2 - (R-r)^2) u^2 ]/(4a^2) = u/(4a^2) [ (r+R-a)^2 (a+R-r) + (a^2 - (R-r)^2) u ].

But u = a+R+r, and note that (r+R-a)^2 = (R+r-a)^2. Also, a^2 - (R-r)^2 = (a-(R-r))(a+(R-r)) = (a-R+r)(a+R-r) = (r+a-R)(a+R-r). And (a+R-r) appears. So we can factor (a+R-r). Then:

y_A^2+Q = u/(4a^2) [ (R+r-a)^2 (a+R-r) + (a+R-r)(a-R+r)(a+R+r) ]? Actually, (a^2 - (R-r)^2) u = (a-R+r)(a+R-r)(a+R+r) = (a+R-r)(a-R+r)u. So:

y_A^2+Q = u/(4a^2) * (a+R-r) [ (R+r-a)^2 + (a-R+r)u ].

But (R+r-a)^2 = (a-(R+r))^2? Actually, R+r-a = (R+r)-a, so square is (a-(R+r))^2. And (a-R+r) = (a+r-R). So we have:

y_A^2+Q = u (a+R-r) [ (a-(R+r))^2 + (a+r-R)(a+R+r) ] / (4a^2).

Now compute inside: (a-(R+r))^2 + (a+r-R)(a+R+r). Expand:

(a-(R+r))^2 = a^2 - 2a(R+r) + (R+r)^2.
(a+r-R)(a+R+r) = (a + (r-R))(a + (R+r))? Actually, (a+r-R)(a+R+r) = (a + (r-R))(a + (R+r)). Expand: = a^2 + a(R+r) + a(r-R) + (r-R)(R+r) = a^2 + a(R+r+r-R) + (r^2 - R^2) = a^2 + 2a r + (r^2 - R^2). So sum = a^2 -2a(R+r) + (R+r)^2 + a^2 + 2a r + (r^2 - R^2) = 2a^2 -2aR -2ar + (R^2+2Rr+r^2) + 2ar + r^2 - R^2 = 2a^2 -2aR + (2Rr + 2r^2?) Wait, cancel: -2aR -2ar + 2ar = -2aR. So sum = 2a^2 -2aR + (R^2+2Rr+r^2) + r^2 - R^2 = 2a^2 -2aR + 2Rr + 2r^2 = 2a^2 -2aR + 2Rr + 2r^2 = 2(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2). That's not a perfect square. But maybe we can factor 2: = 2( a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2). So:

y_A^2+Q = u (a+R-r) * 2( a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2) / (4a^2) = u (a+R-r) (a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2) / (2a^2). So:

y_A^2+Q = (u (a+R-r) (a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2))/(2a^2).   (Equation B)

Now, note that a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2 = (a^2 + r^2 - aR + Rr). This might be factorable with something like (a+r)(a+R? Not.

But we also have from the expression for Δx: Δx = ((R-r)u)/(2a). And also a+R-r appears. So we can express C = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A). Compute a - x_A = a - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (2a^2 - a^2 - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = (a^2 + R^2 - r^2)/(2a). So:

Δx (a - x_A) = ((R-r)u/(2a)) * ((a^2+R^2-r^2)/(2a)) = (R-r)u (a^2+R^2-r^2)/(4a^2).

Now (y_A^2+Q)/2 = (u (a+R-r) (a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2))/(4a^2). So:

C = (y_A^2+Q)/2 + Δx (a - x_A) = u/(4a^2) [ (a+R-r)(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2) + (R-r)(a^2+R^2-r^2) ].

Now compute the bracket:

T = (a+R-r)(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2) + (R-r)(a^2+R^2-r^2).

Expand first term: (a+R-r)(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2). Let’s denote:

(a+R-r) = (a + (R-r)). Multiply: = a*(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2) + (R-r)*(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2). = a^3 - a^2R + aRr + a r^2 + (R-r)a^2 - (R-r)aR + (R-r)Rr + (R-r)r^2.

Now (R-r)a^2 = a^2(R-r). So combine with -a^2R: -a^2R + a^2(R-r) = -a^2R + a^2R - a^2r = -a^2r. Good.

Next, aRr + a r^2: keep. Then - (R-r)aR = -aR(R-r) = -aR^2 + aRr. So combine with aRr: aRr + (-aR^2 + aRr) = -aR^2 + 2aRr. Then also we have (R-r)Rr = Rr(R-r) = R^2r - Rr^2. And (R-r)r^2 = r^2(R-r) = Rr^2 - r^3.

So sum of the two parts: a^3 - a^2r + (-aR^2+2aRr) + a r^2 + (R^2r - Rr^2) + (Rr^2 - r^3). Combine a r^2 + (-Rr^2) + (Rr^2) = a r^2. Then (R^2r - r^3) = r(R^2 - r^2). So we have:

a^3 - a^2r - aR^2 + 2aRr + a r^2 + r(R^2 - r^2).

Now add the second term: (R-r)(a^2+R^2-r^2) = (R-r)a^2 + (R-r)R^2 - (R-r)r^2 = a^2(R-r) + R^2(R-r) - r^2(R-r). So T = [a^3 - a^2r - aR^2 + 2aRr + a r^2 + r(R^2 - r^2)] + [a^2(R-r) + R^2(R-r) - r^2(R-r)].

Now a^3 - a^2r + a^2(R-r) = a^3 - a^2r + a^2R - a^2r = a^3 - 2a^2r + a^2R. But note that -a^2r + a^2(R-r) = a^2(R-2r). So we have a^3 + a^2(R-2r) - aR^2 + 2aRr + a r^2 + r(R^2 - r^2) + R^2(R-r) - r^2(R-r). Combine the terms with R^2: -aR^2 + R^2(R-r) = R^2(R - a - r)? Actually, -aR^2 + R^2(R-r) = R^2(R - a - r). And the terms with r^2: a r^2 - r^2(R-r) = r^2(a - R + r) = r^2(a + r - R). So T = a^3 + a^2(R-2r) + R^2(R - a - r) + r^2(a + r - R) + 2aRr + r(R^2 - r^2). But note that r(R^2 - r^2) = rR^2 - r^3. This is messy.

Given the complexity, maybe we should avoid computing C explicitly. Instead, we can compute the distance condition using the coordinates of H and the line L. Perhaps we can find a more elegant relation: The line through H parallel to AP is the polar of H with respect to circle (BEF) if H lies on the circle? Not.

Let's try a different analytic approach: Use coordinate geometry but shift the origin to the midpoint of MN. Let’s set coordinate system such that the midpoint of MN is at origin. Let M = (-a/2, 0), N = (a/2, 0). Then the x-axis is line MN. The circles: Ω: center M, radius r; Γ: center N, radius R. The radical axis AB is vertical line x = some coordinate. Let's compute coordinates.

Let a = MN = d. Then M = (-d/2, 0), N = (d/2, 0). Points C and D: C is on Ω on the side opposite N, so from M to N direction is positive x. The ray from M away from N is negative x. So C = M + (-r, 0) = (-d/2 - r, 0). D is on Γ on the ray from N away from M, so from N in positive x direction: D = N + (R, 0) = (d/2 + R, 0). Then order: C (-d/2 - r), M (-d/2), N (d/2), D (d/2+R). So order C, M, N, D holds as long as -d/2 - r < -d/2 < d/2 < d/2+R, which is true. Good.

Now A is intersection of circles: |z - (-d/2)| = r and |z - (d/2)| = R. So A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A>0. Then B = (x_A, -y_A). Since circles symmetric about x-axis.

Now P is circumcenter of triangle A, C, D. C = (-d/2 - r, 0), D = (d/2 + R, 0). Midpoint of CD: ((-d/2 - r + d/2 + R)/2, 0) = ((R - r)/2, 0). So x-coordinate of P is (R - r)/2. So P = ((R - r)/2, y_P). Then we can compute y_P from equal distances: PA = PC. Compute PC^2 = (x_P - x_C)^2 + y_P^2 = ((R-r)/2 - (-d/2 - r))^2 + y_P^2 = ((R-r)/2 + d/2 + r)^2 + y_P^2 = ((d/2 + (R-r)/2 + r)^2? Actually, (R-r)/2 + d/2 + r = d/2 + (R-r+2r)/2 = d/2 + (R+r)/2 = (d+R+r)/2. So PC^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + y_P^2.

PA^2 = (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2. Set equal: (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + y_P^2. So (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_P^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2 + y_P^2 => (x_P - x_A)^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2 = ((d+R+r)/2)^2. Thus 2y_P y_A = (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)/2)^2. So y_P = [ (x_P - x_A)^2 + y_A^2 - ((d+R+r)/2)^2 ]/(2y_A). That's similar.

Now AP vector: v = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). We can compute Δx = x_P - x_A = (R - r)/2 - x_A. And Δy = y_P - y_A.

First, define:

a = MN = d.

Then Δx = ((R-r)u)/(2a).   (1)

Now compute Q = (α+r)(a+R-α). We have α+r = (a^2+r^2-R^2+2ar)/(2a) = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)/(2a) = ((a+r-R)(a+r+R))/(2a) = ((a+r-R)u)/(2a).
And a+R-α = (a+R) - (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a) = (2a(a+R) - (a^2+r^2-R^2))/(2a) = (a^2+2aR - r^2 + R^2)/(2a) = ((a+R)^2 - r^2)/(2a) = ((a+R-r)(a+R+r))/(2a) = ((a+R-r)u)/(2a).
Thus Q = ((a+r-R)u)/(2a) * ((a+R-r)u)/(2a) = ( (a+r-R)(a+R-r) u^2 )/(4a^2). And note that (a+r-R)(a+R-r) = (a+(r-R))(a+(R-r)) = a^2 - (R-r)^2 = a^2 - (R-r)^2. So Q = (a^2 - (R-r)^2) u^2/(4a^2).

Now compute y_A^2 = β^2 = r^2 - α^2 = ((a+r)^2 - R^2)(R^2 - (a-r)^2)/(4a^2). But we can also compute β^2 = r^2 - ((a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a))^2 = (4a^2r^2 - (a^2+r^2-R^2)^2)/(4a^2). We'll keep β^2 as is.

But we then simplified T to something? We computed T = 2(a^2 - aR + Rr + r^2)? No, we didn't simplify completely. Let's re-derive that step carefully.

We had:

Another approach: Use vector geometry with barycentric coordinates. Let’s denote the circumcenter of triangle ACD as P. Then P is the point such that PA = PC = PD. So P is the center of the circle through A, C, D. Since C and D are on MN, we can write P as the intersection of the perpendicular bisector of CD (which is line through midpoint of CD and perpendicular to MN) and the perpendicular bisector of AC. This is not simpler.

Given the difficulty, I'll attempt to produce a coordinate proof but with a systematic simplification using the expressions we derived. I'll compute O using the system of linear equations and then compute the distance condition. I will do the algebra step by step, using the expressions in terms of a, r, R, and u = a+R+r, and also using the expressions for s_E, Δx, Δy, etc. I'll try to simplify the algebra by factoring common terms.

Let's define:

a = MN = d.
r = OM, R = ON, with r<R.
Define:
x_A = α = (a^2+r^2-R^2)/(2a).
y_A = β = √(r^2-α^2) > 0.
Then B = (α, -β).

Define:
u = a+R+r.
Δx = ((R-r)u)/(2a).   (1)

Now compute Q = (α+r)(a+R-α). And we have from earlier: y_A^2+Q = (a+R-α) * (R+r)(a+r-R)/a. Let's compute that:

Let Y = a+R-α. Then y_A^2+Q = Y * (R+r)(a+r-R)/a.

Given the time, I'll produce a solution that uses coordinate geometry but with a clear algebraic verification. I'll present the steps and then show that the distance condition holds using the expressions we derived. I'll include the algebra in a concise way.

I'll start:

Set up coordinate system: Let M = (0,0), N = (d,0) with d = MN > 0. Let the radii be r = OM, R = ON with r < R and circles intersect, so |R-r| < d < R+r. Let A = (x_A, y_A) with y_A > 0 be the intersection with positive y-coordinate. Then B = (x_A, -y_A). Compute:

x_A = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d), y_A^2 = r^2 - x_A^2.

Let u = d + R + r. Then the line AP is defined. Compute P as circumcenter of ACD. Since C = (-r,0), D = (d+R,0), the midpoint of CD is ((d+R-r)/2, 0). So P = (x_P, y_P) with x_P = (d+R-r)/2. From PA = PC, we get:

y_P = (y_A^2 - (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A))/(2y_A) = -(y_A^2 + (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A))/(2y_A).   (1)

Let Q = (x_A+r)(d+R-x_A). Then y_P = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). Also, note that y_A^2+Q = (d+R-x_A)*(R+r)(d+r-R)/d. (Derived earlier.)

Now the vector v = AP = (x_P - x_A, y_P - y_A). Compute:

Δx = x_P - x_A = ((R-r)(d+R+r))/(2d) = ((R-r)u)/(2d).   (2)

Δy = y_P - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A) - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q+2y_A^2)/(2y_A) = -(3y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). (3)

But we can also use the expression for y_P to get Δy = -(y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A) - y_A = -(y_A^2+Q+2y_A^2)/(2y_A) = -(3y_A^2+Q)/(2y_A). So that's fine.

Now the line AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. Parameterize: A + s v. Then:

s_E = -2(x_AΔx + y_AΔy)/(Δx^2+Δy^2), s_F = -2((x_A-d)Δx + y_AΔy)/(Δx^2+Δy^2). Since (x_A-d)Δx + y_AΔy = x_AΔx + y_AΔy - dΔx.

Thus s_F = s_E + 2dΔx/(Δx^2+Δy^2).   (4)

Now define points:

E = (x_A + s_EΔx, y_A + s_EΔy), F = (x_A + s_FΔx, y_A + s_FΔy).

Now we consider the circumcircle of triangle BEF. Let its equation be:

X^2+Y^2 + AX + BY + C = 0.   (5)

Since B = (x_A, -y_A) lies on it, we have:

x_A^2 + y_A^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0 => r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0.   (6)

Since E lies on it, we have:

(x_A + s_EΔx)^2 + (y_A + s_EΔy)^2 + A(x_A + s_EΔx) + B(y_A + s_EΔy) + C = 0.
Using (6) and expanding, we get:

2s_E(x_AΔx + y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + A s_EΔx + B s_EΔy = 0.
Dividing by s_E (since s_E ≠ 0), we have:

2(x_AΔx + y_AΔy) + s_E(Δx^2+Δy^2) + AΔx + BΔy = 0.   (7)

Similarly, for F:

2(x_AΔx + y_AΔy) + s_F(Δx^2+Δy^2) + AΔx + BΔy = 0.   (8)

Subtracting (7) from (8) gives:

(s_F - s_E)(Δx^2+Δy^2) = 0, but s_F ≠ s_E. So this seems contradictory unless we made a mistake: The equations for E and F should both hold, and they are linear in A and B. They give two equations:

AΔx + BΔy = -2(x_AΔx + y_AΔy) - s_E(Δx^2+Δy^2).   (7')
AΔx + BΔy = -2(x_AΔx + y_AΔy) - s_F(Δx^2+Δy^2).   (8')

For these to be consistent, we must have s_E = s_F. So our derivation is wrong: The expansion for E should include the term B y_A? Let's redo the expansion for E correctly, keeping track of B y_A.

We have circle equation: X^2+Y^2 + AX + BY + C = 0.

For point E: X_E = x_A + s_E Δx, Y_E = y_A + s_E Δy.

So:

X_E^2+Y_E^2 = (x_A + s_EΔx)^2 + (y_A + s_EΔy)^2 = x_A^2+2x_A s_EΔx + s_E^2Δx^2 + y_A^2+2y_A s_EΔy + s_E^2Δy^2 = r^2 + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2).

Then A X_E + B Y_E = A(x_A+s_EΔx) + B(y_A+s_EΔy) = A x_A + A s_EΔx + B y_A + B s_EΔy.

So the circle equation becomes:

r^2 + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + A x_A + A s_EΔx + B y_A + B s_EΔy + C = 0.

Now from (6): r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0 => r^2 + A x_A + C = B y_A.

Substitute that into the equation: (r^2 + A x_A + C) + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + B y_A + A s_EΔx + B s_EΔy = 0? Wait, we have B y_A from the expansion, and also we have C already used. Actually, the expression is:

r^2 + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + A x_A + A s_EΔx + B y_A + B s_EΔy + C = 0.

Group r^2 + A x_A + C + B y_A + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + A s_EΔx + B s_EΔy = 0.

But r^2 + A x_A + C = B y_A from (6). So:

B y_A + B y_A + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + A s_EΔx + B s_EΔy = 0 => 2B y_A + 2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) + s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2) + A s_EΔx + B s_EΔy = 0.

Thus:

A s_EΔx + B (2y_A + s_EΔy) = -2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) - s_E^2(Δx^2+Δy^2).   (7')

Similarly, for F:

A s_FΔx + B (2y_A + s_FΔy) = -2s_F(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) - s_F^2(Δx^2+Δy^2).   (8')

These are two linear equations in A and B. They are consistent because the coefficients are different. So we can solve for A and B. Now, we can compute the center O = (-A/2, -B/2). Then we need to show that the line through H parallel to v is tangent to the circle. That is, distance from O to line L = radius R_c = sqrt(A^2/4 + B^2/4 - C). Since we have C from (6): C = -r^2 - A x_A + B y_A.

Now, we can compute the distance from O to line L. The line L passes through H = (x_P, y_H) with direction v. So its equation can be written as: (X - x_P)Δx + (Y - y_H)Δy = 0? No, that's the equation of the line through H with direction v? Actually, the line through H with direction v has parametric form H + t v. Its equation can be written as: (X - x_P)/Δx = (Y - y_H)/Δy, assuming Δx≠0. So the equation is: Δy (X - x_P) - Δx (Y - y_H) = 0 => Δy X - Δx Y + (Δx y_H - Δy x_P) = 0. So the distance from O = (X_O, Y_O) to this line is |Δy X_O - Δx Y_O + (Δx y_H - Δy x_P)| / sqrt(Δx^2+Δy^2) = |Δy X_O - Δx Y_O + D| / |v|, where D = Δx y_H - Δy x_P.

Now, the radius R_c = sqrt(A^2/4 + B^2/4 - C). But we can also express the power of H with respect to the circle: Power(H) = |H-O|^2 - R_c^2. If the line through H with direction v is tangent, then the distance from O to the line equals R_c, which is equivalent to: The line is tangent iff the power of H equals the square of the distance from H to the line? Not exactly: The condition for a line through H to be tangent is that the foot of perpendicular from O to the line is at distance R_c. That gives a relation.

Alternatively, we can compute the discriminant of the quadratic in t when substituting H + t v into the circle equation. That discriminant must be zero for tangency. The equation of the circle: X^2+Y^2+AX+BY+C=0. For point H + t v, we have:

|H + t v|^2 + A(H_x + tΔx) + B(H_y + tΔy) + C = 0 => (|H|^2 + 2t H·v + t^2|v|^2) + A H_x + A tΔx + B H_y + B tΔy + C = 0 => (|H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C) + 2t (H·v) + t^2|v|^2 + A tΔx + B tΔy = 0 => (|H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C) + t (2H·v + AΔx + BΔy) + t^2|v|^2 = 0.

So the quadratic in t is: |v|^2 t^2 + (2H·v + AΔx + BΔy) t + (|H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C) = 0.

For the line to be tangent, this quadratic must have a double root, i.e., its discriminant = 0. So:

(2H·v + AΔx + BΔy)^2 - 4|v|^2 (|H|^2 + A H_x + B H_y + C) = 0.   (9)

Now, we can compute the left side using the expressions for A, B, C in terms of the points. This might simplify if we use the fact that the circle passes through B, E, F, and that H is orthocenter of PMN.

We have H = (x_P, y_H) with y_H = x_P(d-x_P)/y_P. And we have expressions for x_P, y_P, etc. Also, v = (Δx, Δy) with Δx = x_P - x_A, Δy = y_P - y_A. So H·v = x_P Δx + y_H Δy. And |v|^2 = PA^2.

Also, from the circle equation, we have that for point B: r^2 + A x_A - B y_A + C = 0. For point E: we derived that A and B satisfy a linear system. We can try to compute A and B explicitly using the expressions for s_E, etc. But maybe we can find a relation between A, B, and the parameters.

Let's try to compute A and B from the system (7') and (8'). That is:

s_EΔx * A + (2y_A + s_EΔy) * B = -2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) - s_E^2|v|^2.   (7')
s_FΔx * A + (2y_A + s_FΔy) * B = -2s_F(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) - s_F^2|v|^2.   (8')

We can solve for A and B using Cramer's rule. The determinant of the coefficient matrix is:

Δ = s_EΔx (2y_A + s_FΔy) - s_FΔx (2y_A + s_EΔy) = s_EΔx (2y_A + s_FΔy) - s_FΔx (2y_A + s_EΔy) = 2y_A (s_EΔx - s_FΔx) + s_E s_F Δx Δy - s_F s_E Δx Δy = 2y_A Δx (s_E - s_F). So Δ = 2y_A Δx (s_E - s_F).

Now, s_E - s_F = -2dΔx/|v|^2 (from (4)). So s_E - s_F = -2dΔx/|v|^2. Thus Δ = 2y_A Δx * (-2dΔx/|v|^2) = -4y_A d Δx^2/|v|^2.

Now, we can compute the numerator for A: A_num = det( [-2s_E(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) - s_E^2|v|^2, 2y_A + s_EΔy; -2s_F(x_AΔx+y_AΔy) - s_F^2|v|^2, 2y_A + s_FΔy] ]. This is a 2x2 determinant. Let's denote:

P = x_AΔx+y_AΔy. Then the first column entries: -2s_E P - s_E^2|v|^2, -2s_F P - s_F^2|v|^2. Second column: 2y_A + s_EΔy, 2y_A + s_FΔy.

So A_num = (-2s_E P - s_E^2|v|^2)(2y_A + s_FΔy) - (-2s_F P - s_F^2|v|^2)(2y_A + s_EΔy). = (-2s_E P - s_E^2|v|^2)(2y_A + s_FΔy) + (2s_F P + s_F^2|v|^2)(2y_A + s_EΔy).

=> Δx (s_F-s_E) A + Δy (s_F-s_E) B = -2(s_F-s_E)P - (s_F-s_E)(s_F+s_E)|v|^2.
Divide by (s_F-s_E) (non-zero): Δx A + Δy B = -2P - (s_F+s_E)|v|^2.   (11)

So we have a simple expression: AΔx + BΔy = -2P - (s_E+s_F)|v|^2.

Γ: (x - a/2)^2 + y^2 = R^2.

From these, we can compute x_A and y_A. Subtract: (x - a/2)^2 - (x + a/2)^2 = R^2 - r^2 => (x^2 - a x + a^2/4) - (x^2 + a x + a^2/4) = R^2 - r^2 => -2a x = R^2 - r^2 => x_A = (r^2 - R^2)/(2a). So x_A = -(R^2 - r^2)/(2a). That's simpler! Good.

Then from Ω: (x_A + a/2)^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => ( (r^2 - R^2)/(2a) + a/2 )^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => ( (r^2 - R^2 + a^2)/(2a) )^2 + y_A^2 = r^2 => y_A^2 = r^2 - ((a^2 + r^2 - R^2)^2)/(4a^2). That's the same as before.

Now C is on Ω on the side opposite N. Since M is at (-a/2,0), the line MN is x-axis. The ray from M away from N is leftwards (negative x). So C = M + (-r, 0) = (-a/2 - r, 0). D is on Γ on the ray from N away from M, so D = N + (R, 0) = (a/2 + R, 0). So order: C (-a/2 - r), M (-a/2), N (a/2), D (a/2+R). So that's consistent.

Now the midpoint of CD: (( -a/2 - r + a/2 + R)/2, 0) = ((R - r)/2, 0). So P = ((R-r)/2, y_P). From PA = PC:

PC^2 = (x_P - x_C)^2 + y_P^2 = ( (R-r)/2 - (-a/2 - r) )^2 + y_P^2 = ( (R-r)/2 + a/2 + r )^2 + y_P^2 = ( (a + R + r)/2 )^2 + y_P^2 = ((a+R+r)/2)^2 + y_P^2.

PA^2 = (x_P - x_A)^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2 = ( (R-r)/2 - x_A )^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2.

Set equal: ((a+R+r)/2)^2 + y_P^2 = ( (R-r)/2 - x_A )^2 + (y_P - y_A)^2.

((R-r)/2 - x_A)^2 = ((R-r)^2 (a+R+r)^2)/(4a^2).

=> ((a+R+r)^2)/4 + y_P^2 = ((R-r)^2 (a+R+r)^2)/(4a^2) + y_P^2 - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2.

Cancel y_P^2: ((a+R+r)^2)/4 = ((R-r)^2 (a+R+r)^2)/(4a^2) - 2y_P y_A + y_A^2.

[2H·v + 2(P - aΔx)]^2 - 4|v|^2 Power_H = 0 => 4[ H·v + (P - aΔx) ]^2 - 4|v|^2 Power_H = 0 => [ H·v + (P - aΔx) ]^2 = |v|^2 Power_H.
